0439023513|4.0|Mockingjay, by acclaimed author Suzanne Collins, who also authored the Underland Chronicles and worked as a scriptwriter for various children's programs throughout her career, is the third and thrilling conclusion to the highly praised Hunger Games Trilogy. The series is inspired heavily by the Greek myth of Theseus and the Minotaur, and the barbaric animosity of the Roman Gladiator games. In the first book of the series, two children ranging from the ages of twelve to eighteen are selected to participate in the games in a fight to the death. In Catching Fire, the book shows what occurs to the main characters after they are forced to take part in their second Hunger Games. Now, as the conclusion to this breath-taking series arrives, in the first person viewpoint of the chivalrous protagonist Katniss Everdeen, whose tone and voice are as engaging as when we first encountered her in The Hunger Games, we see whether or not if some things are worth fighting for. Published by Scholastic Press, the book I am reviewing is 390 pages long, separated into three parts with nine chapters each for a total of 27, and contains no illustrations.<br />Taking place immediately after Catching Fire, in a future where North America exists as a post-apocalyptic country called Panem consisting of a rich Capitol and twelve (previously thirteen) surrounding districts, the protagonists Katniss Everdeen and Peeta Mellark have been separated. Katniss is rescued abruptly by the forces of the presumably annihilated District 13 and reunited with her sweet sister Prim, her mother, and her best friend, Gale, with who she is starting to harbor feelings towards. Meanwhile, Peeta is taken in by the clutches of the Capitol, the oppressive, leading force that Katniss is rebelling against, and interrogated by President Snow, the sadistic and psychopathic leader of the Capitol. Mockingjay has rich imaginative characters that serve as an example of the weaknesses of the human condition, and ultimately adds to the overall grandeur of the story.<br />  During the 75th Hunger Games in which Katniss Everdeen and Peeta Mellark are forced to take part in as they are the only eligible and capable victors from their district, Katniss is rescued by the forces of District 13, while Peeta is held captive by the Capitol. While in the underground dwelling that District 13 has taken to constructing, Katniss is told that she must become a symbol of rebellion against the Capitol, and infiltrate their defenses. During the course of the book, Peeta is rescued and brought to District 13, but is very different than what Katniss remembers. As the rebels begin to orchestrate their final assault on the Capitol, many lives are lost in the kerfuffle, and Katniss has to choose whether or not some people are worth trusting. Even your enemies.<br />  The themes that this book illustrates are hopefulness, such as in the face of war. Katniss serves as an icon of rebellion against the Capitol, and seeks freedom from their oppressive rule. Mockingjay demonstrates the concepts of betrayal, and how it's dealt with when others loyalties are hard to pinpoint. The morality of the decision of others are a force to be reckoned with, as Katniss also struggles to discover if her own choices are right or wrong. Another theme that is demonstrated in the book is perseverance, which Katniss has done ever since the death of her father to keep her family alive. Although she is faced with difficult choices and has the weight of an entire population on her shoulders, she perseveres and keeps walking towards the path that she thinks is right.<br />  Overall, I thought that this book was an amazing read. Suzanne Collin's voice keeps you engaged and excited for whatever lies on the next page. The ending wasn't completely satisfying, and at first I felt feelings of exasperation and bewilderment as I read the final sentence. However, after thinking about the events that had transpired and the overall setting and tone ofthe book, I came to the realization that in war it's highly improbable that everyone you know and love would all survive through it. Because of that realization, the story seemed to hold a greater impact on than it would if all the characters survived. This book captures the realistic views on war, and doesn't bother to hide the details with purple prose. The reality of the character's situations gives it a greater appeal, and people who have been through the harshness of war will give Mockingjay a commendable nod.e to the realization that in war it's highly improbable that everyone you know and love would all survive through it. B0000027D5|4.0|`Lotus' was a live concert recording in Japan of the same line-up that made the excellent (but at the time underrated) `Welcome' studio album, billed as `The New Santana Band.' The concerts from which the material was taken were on 3rd & 4th July 1973, and the album was first released in May the following year.<br /><br />The original release was a triple 12-inch vinyl offering with a predominantly black fold-out cover like an enormous work of origami, festooned with colourful religious imagery from Buddhism, Hinduism and Christianity to symbolise spiritual union. It was uniquely and extravagantly lavish not just in the history of Santana's prolific releases over the years but for any other music from the era, and has become collectible in itself. Both 3CD and 2CD releases followed with broadly identical (but of course much smaller) replicas of the original album artwork.<br /><br />The most common format available now is the 2CD, though you can still track down 3-disk vinyl originals in prime condition.<br /><br />The music of the `New Santana Band' from this period is predominantly jazz-rock fusion fed by Latin rhythms and complex percussion from Armando Peraza and Jose Chepito Areas, with Michael Shrieve on drums. The sound is predominantly like that on `Caravanserai' and also more keyboard-driven (the excellent duo of Tom Coster and Richard Kermode) than the Woodstock-era Santana. `Lotus' features a couple of numbers from the forthcoming `Welcome' album performed ahead of release, and apart from a powerful performance of `Every Step of the Way' from `Caravanserai' most of the rest of the material is from the band's first three albums. Leon Thomas walks on and off stage to sing a couple of numbers but is on the whole under-employed.<br /><br />The band is tight and bursting with energy, with some real virtuoso playing. It's great to hear the line-up which produced the ambient, jazz-leaning `Welcome' album tearing into Santana classics like `Black Magic Woman/Gypsy Queen', `Incident at Neshabur' (a real stormer on this album) and `Toussaint L'Overture' with gusto and outdoing anything the earlier incarnation of the band ever delivered onstage, great though they were.<br /><br />Detractors who like to hear Santana play tight and disciplined `songs' sometimes complain about the long extended pieces, too much jamming, Carlos' guitar taking a back seat on some numbers, a long indulgent drum solo from Michael Shrieve (well, this was the 1970s folks) and less-than-perfect sound recording. The only gripe with any merit might be that the drum solo could have been excluded from the final album release, but to be honest the other criticisms don't have much substance. Carlos is in great form (especially on his extended and imaginative delivery of `Samba Pa Ti') though does not personally dominate the music: he allows space for his fellow band members to shine. The sound recording is clear and nicely balanced too, for a live concert from any era let alone the 1970s.<br /><br />Overall `Lotus' is a great album and something of a landmark, but will appeal mostly to fans of the band's more imaginative output during this productive period, when Santana explored jazz fusion and IMO made their best-ever music. It might appeal less to those who like the band only when they play structured five-minute songs, so if this more mellow side of Santana is what floats your boat, then best leave `Lotus' well alone.y Queen', `Incident at Neshabur' (a real stormer on this album) and `Toussaint L'Overture' with gusto and outdoing anything the earlier incarnation of the band ever delivered onstage, great though they were.<br /><br />Detractors who like to hear Santana play tight and disciplined `songs' sometimes complain about the long extended pieces, too much jamming, Carlos' guitar taking a back seat on some numbers, a long indulgent drum solo from Michael Shrieve (well, this was the 1970s folks) and less-than-perfect sound recording. The only gripe with any merit might be that the drum solo could have been excluded from the final album release, but to be honest the other criticisms don't have much substance. Carlos is in great form (especially on his extended and imaginative delivery of `Samba Pa Ti') though does not personally dominate the music: he allows space for his fellow band members to shine. The sound recording is clear and nicely balanced too, for a live concert from any era let alone the 1970s.<br /><br />Overall `Lotus' is a great album and something of a landmark, but will appeal mostly to fans of the band's more imaginative output during this productive period, when Santana explored jazz fusion and IMO made their best-ever music. It might appeal less to those who like the band only when they play structured five-minute songs, so if this more mellow side of Santana is what floats your boat, then best leave `Lotus' well alone.
B003INBNXO|2.0|I'm a bit stunned that folks rate this so highly. I'm not talking about its merits as a set of fight scenes, which are mostly great. But you can't just ignore all the irresponsible crap that surrounds these scenes. I'm talking about the fact that Ip Man is just another in a recent string of shameless big-budget propaganda-like films coming out of China (\\"Hero\\" is another example, though Ip Man is much more blatant and troubling).  Look, normally I would be the last person to overanalyze a kung fu flick, let alone chastise filmmakers for taking on historical atrocities. But this movie sees itself as much more than just a kung fu flick, and that's the problem. I realize that what the Japanese did to the Chinese before/during WWII was horrific. And I also understand that U.S. studios are just as capable of putting out pugilistic propaganda as the Chinese (Rocky IV, anyone?). But there are important differences that make Ip Man truly shameless and worthy of criticism, from its blatant hypocrisy (the autocratic Chinese lecturing the Japanese about being \\"civilized\\" when they massacre their own people in Tiananmen Square and are consistently ranked among the world's top oppressors by numerous human-rights organizations? You've GOT to be kidding me!) to near-delusional historical revisionism (at the end of the movie, the narrator proudly declares that the Chinese forced the Japanese surrender in 1945!) to implicit racism (the movie doesn't just criticize past Japanese imperialism; it also tries to make a case for intrinsic Chinese superiority, then and now). The fact that top-shelf actors and martial artists lent their talents to such an enterprise, and that many reviewers are praising them for doing so, is truly disturbing to me.  So for those of you who love good kung fu, I recommend just looking up the good fight scenes individually on YTube instead of sitting through all the propaganda BS. And for those of you wanting to see a thoughtful examination of the Japanese occupation of China, for the love of god look elsewhere!

0143034901|4.0|I enjoy lush descriptions of setting and the social milieu of a place and time if there is a page-turning plot; and Carlos Ruiz Zafon gives the reader that.  There is plenty of plot and atmosphere.  There is plenty for a fantasy-buff to like.  The idea is the best place to start for my list of likes: the eponymous book that is a mystery in itself, the love story, the obsession of the writer, Julian Carax.<br /><br />It is an entertaining tale set in Barcelona of a few decades ago, but not the sunny postcard Barcelona.  Rather it is a gothic city of phantoms, haunted houses, love affairs gone wrong, vengeful lovers, improbable twists of fate, ghostly libraries and even more spectral authors.<br /><br />The story is the search of teenaged Daniel, smitten by a book (named \\"Shadow of the Wind\\"), for the story about its author, Julian Carax.  Daniel's father who owns a bookshop took him to the Cemetery of Forgotten Books for this rare copy of the book.  Daniel discovers that Carax is the author of several books, which despite having been commercial failures, are being destroyed by a mysterious person.<br /><br />As Daniel also looks for his true love, he finds out about the tortured love life of the mysterious Julian Carax, and his life seems to be running parallel to his hero's, down torturously winding twists and turns.  The background is a character for a horror story: mists, smoke, dark alleys, midnight trysts, blood and dampness.  It is often convoluted and overwrought in its plot rending, emotions and descriptions of places and objects.  Having said that, this is also what I found to be Zafon's voice.  It is part of what makes his style unique, and in many cases, loved.<br /><br />SHADOW OF THE WIND is not a literary book; it is a fable, a fairy story, a story about an author and a book within a book, and enjoyable for all of that.

0143034901|1.0|The book was translated by Elizabeth Graves, the daughter of the great Robert Graves.<br /><br />Translations are most difficult mainly because the metaphor does not move easily from one language to another. Spanish presents special problems because of a cultural bias toward excusing one's actions.<br /><br />We say &quot;It's raining cats and dogs&quot; but the literal translation of the Spanish equivalent would be &quot;It's raining umbrellas.&quot; It is important to understand that there always three levels of translation and it takes a very insightful translator to know which is appropriate word by word, phrase by phrase, etc.<br /><br />We say, &quot;I dropped the pencil&quot; but the literal Spanish equivalent would be &quot;The pencil fell to the floor in relation to me.&quot; This is a reflexive verb endemic to a language where cultural givens absolve people for responsibility of their actions because predestination is the assumption. Some people think of this as baby-talk but it is certainly an unsophisticated view of the universe.<br /><br />We say, &quot;What is your name?&quot; The Spanish equivalent is &quot;How are you called?&quot;; &quot;How old are you?&quot; in Spanish comes out &quot;How many years have you?&quot;<br /><br />Robert Graves was one of the greatest scholars of all time. It is a shame his daughter tackled a job she was not adequate to do.<br /><br />However, there is much more to the main problem with the story than translation though it is difficult to know for sure what would have been a better way to deal with the story other than a really good editor holding the writer's feet to the fire.<br /><br />Simply put, the first person narrator knows things he can't possibly know. This is prevalent in modern/post-modern fiction where both writers and readers falsely assume that a first person narrator who is by nature subjective can tell a story objectively.<br /><br />Robert Graves' narrator of I, CLAUDIUS and CLAUDIUS THE GOD is the greatest sustained first person narrator in all of literature as far as I know. The brilliance of it is its form, an unreliable narrator. We can't believe a word Claudius says and in that the story becomes more than its actions proper, and the perception of that by the reader is what every writer who knows point of view should strive for.<br /><br />Masters of the form are Henry James (TURN OF THE SCREW), Ford Maddox Ford (THE GOOD SOLDIER), Ring Lardner (&quot;Haircut&quot; et al.), Ken Kesey (ONE FLEW OVER THE CUCKOO'S NEST), Truman Capote (&quot;Jug of Silver&quot;), H. G. Wells (&quot;The Magic Shop&quot;), Poe (&quot;The Black Cat&quot;), and Peter Taylor (&quot;Hand of Emmagene&quot; and anything else of his work), the greatest master of first person narratives of all time. Many other stories could be cited but perhaps the variety of these is sufficient for those who might want to pursue the matter.<br /><br />SHADOW OF THE WIND could have been an enduring work of art. I grieve that it had a weak editor who really ought to be flayed, whipped, and shot.Claudius says and in that the story becomes more than its actions proper, and the perception of that by the reader is what every writer who knows point of view should strive for.  Masters of the form are Henry James (TURN OF THE SCREW), Ford Maddox Ford (THE GOOD SOLDIER), Ring Lardner (\\"Haircut\\" et al.), Ken Kesey (ONE FLEW OVER THE CUCKOO'S NEST), Truman Capote (\\"Jug of Silver\\"), H. 1451648537|5.0|Walter Isaacson's Steve Jobs is a well-written source about one of the world's greatest entrepreneurs and innovators. Though the man portrayed in this volume is frequently unlikable to the reader who peruses through each page, no one will honestly deny his fascinating drive and genius which helped propel Apple to the status of a high-tech company with a market capitalization that very recently eclipsed the $400 billion mark.  Quite interesting is the level of depth in the research that Mr. Isaacson took on to author this best-selling project. He travelled many miles to various homes, museums and business meetings to explore not only Steve Jobs the successful businessman but Steve Jobs the complex individual. Often highlighted are the notorious temper tantrums and controlling behaviors that would create personal and professional rivalries from the beginning of Apple's days in the garage to Jobs' final days on Earth. From analyzing the character of Mr. Jobs, especially when Apple first went public, and then documenting the recollections and eyewitness accounts of the physical, financial, and emotional havoc it could impose upon those nearest him, Isaacson provides a glimpse of the downside of the corporate world. Regarding what it must have been like for those who assisted Jobs in laying the groundwork and lighting the many sparks that would enable Apple to evolve from a million-dollar to a multi-billion dollar business, the exasperated reader cannot help but say,\\"Wow. A case could be made that, on average, Steve Jobs treated his own friends worse than he did his worst enemies.\\"  According to what Isaacson could gather, interspersed with Jobs' Type A tendencies were the recurring, antisocial patterns that would come to portray the worst of Steve Jobs. Mentioned were the unprofessional job interviews conducted to embarrass job-seeking college graduates, the quick willingness to be ruthlessly critical of others, the LSD trips taken, the questionable hygiene habits, thenegligence of responsible parenting, and the unconscionable act of parking in handicapped spots (sometimes two spaces for one drive-in!). All of these, along with other testaments, caused many to perceive that Steve Jobs believed that he was an exception to the dictates of society. Even though it was purported that Steve Jobs died at a young 56, it is from reading the earlier chapters that one almost considers it a miracle that he even surpassed half that age.  Despite Jobs' interpersonal flaws, it is perhaps ironic that what were Jobs' aforementioned weaknesses in communicating with people, both individually and in small groups, turned out to be strengths in terms of contributing to society.  One might say that what might have marred his reputation in a small, short-term scale ended up contributing to an enduring, if not immortal, legacy in the long run.  Indeed, the perpetual \\"You are a genius!\\" to the \\"You are an idiot!\\" tirades were infectious and under different management would altogether destroy not only morale among employees but businesses as a whole. But what was different about Steve Jobs that separated him from most? Well, from Isaacson's information-gathering regarding Steve Jobs' colleagues and employees, both past and present, it was the main ingredient referred to as Jobs' \\"reality distortion field.\\"  According to Isaacson, there are several occurrences where Jobs made demands upon designers and engineers that seemed to defy physical principles and laws, as well as any other items that constituted reality. After caving in to the potential threats, these same workers would to their amazement meet or surpass what they deemed impossible. More than once was it implied or inferred that Jobs was almost like a magician who could, if necessary, bend facts and truths to fit his vision of the world. Remarkably, many of these same people who dreaded the confrontations with Jobs would conclude that their boss man pushed them to heights beyond what they ever imagined.  Without going into too much further detail, Isaacson portrays Steve Jobs as a man of paradoxes that intertwined to make him reputably the best CEO of at least his generation. His seemingly brutal approach  in the workplace nonetheless enforced the development of high-tech gadgets that we, as consumers, have come to experience. Time and again, his business acumen was supplemented by his iron will to have the operations of his company run by A-level players, as well as making, from both an efficient and artistic standpoint, the best products possible.  Throughout, there are recurring themes that made Steve Jobs' Apple thrive, one of which seems to thumb its nose at typical corporate ideologies. It is this: Apple is about the quality of the product, not the potential profitability. Along with the quality of the product would be a continual issue with Jobs, and it was the enjoyability of the user experience of the products with his name on it. From this latter obsession is the continual if not continuous reminder that Jobs came to live by; it is the simple yet philosophical phrase \\"Simplicity is the ultimate sophistication.\\" Quite stark is the passion Steve Jobs had for carrying out his abstract yet sometimes concrete mission of trying to make science and literature meld by making technology meet the liberal arts.  In conclusion, there is so much more that I can add, but I will end by saying that I enthusiastically give Isaacson's work five stars and was quite fascinated by the evolving duality between an open system according to Bill Gates' Microsoft and the like and the closed, integrated, end-to-end system that Jobs wanted Apple to embody.

B00000AFHM|4.0|Released in 1998, THE BOY WITH THE ARAB STRAP was the third album by Scottish group Belle and Sebastian. It mainly continues the twee rock of earlier efforts, centred around Stuart Murdoch's intricate lyrics.<br /><br />Immediately one notices superior production values to anything the band has done before: this album sounds great. The use of bagpipe and electronic loops on the second track and a lush string backdrop on the eighth adds a complexity above the two earlier albums. I would place \\"Sleep the Clock Around\\", \\"Dirty Dream Number 2\\" and the title track among Belle and Sebastian's catchiest songs. \\"Summer Wasting\\" and \\"Simple Things\\" evoke the most intricate of retro pop tunes. Stuart Murdoch's singing is as entertaining as ever. There's some sly dirty humour, an elegy on the difficult of fitting in with one's peers, and never-before-heard introspective moments.<br /><br />As enjoyable as most of the tracks are, THE BOY WITH THE ARAB STRAP is flawed by Murdoch's decision to open up the songwriting process to his bandmates. \\"Seymour Stein\\" and \\"Chickfactor\\" are written and sung by Steve Jackson, and \\"Is It Wicked Not to Care?\\" by Isobel Duncan. I feel the album suffers from their inclusion. They are not necessarily bad songs, and fans of Saturday Looks Good to Me may find Jackson's contributions enjoyable, but they don't jive with the rest of the album, and besides, one listens to Belle and Sebastian for Murdoch's unique storytelling.<br /><br />Still, for listeners exploring Belle and Sebastian's output chronologically (which I'd recommend), this third album has many pleasures and is competitive against its predecessors.

B00275EGWY|2.0|Set in modern Baghdad, The Hurt Locker follows the lives of three of the US Army's bomb disposal technicians as they battle insurgent bombers and their own nerves to stay alive in the world's most dangerous war zone.<br /><br />The Hurt Locker was directed by Kathryn Bigelow ([[ASIN:B000H8RW88 Blue Steel]], [[ASIN:B0050BHM1O Point Break]]) and, released in 2008 to much fanfare and critical acclaim, it was nominated for nine Oscars and eventually won six. It's hard, now, to see what the fuss was about. It is a fairly conventional war movie, typical Hollywood fare in fact and I can see nothing about it that lifts above the herd and it is certainly not the equal of, for instance, [[ASIN:B003LVZYL0 Saving Private Ryan]], [[ASIN:B00004CXLA Das Boot]] or [[ASIN:B00004W4I5 Platoon]].<br /><br />Yes, it's well shot and composed, effectively conveying the dusty, sweaty, grimy life of a soldier in Iraq. Indeed, there are some award winning individual shots (the slo-mo explosion sequence where the dust jumps off the roof of a car is particularly tasty) but the ensemble is barely above the ordinary - [[ASIN:B00004W4FX Three Kings]] did it just as well.<br /><br />It's well acted. Jeremy Renner does a fine job as the maverick bomb disposal tech (you may be able to guess where I'm going to go with this), Anthony Mackie and Brian Geraghty convince as his strung-out support crew and there are some surprising (and brief) cameos from Ralph Fiennes, David Morse and Guy Pearce. But, again, the acting is merely convincing: steely eyes, square jaws, head-in-hands-despair... all that sort of thing.<br /><br />The story, I'm afraid, is what lets the film down. The word \\"maverick\\" really should be banned from use in Hollywood these days ([[ASIN:B00004TT89 Tom Cruise and Tony Scott]] have so much to answer for). It really does a profound disservice to the exeptional bravery and professionalism of bomb disposal technicians of all nationalities to see the lead character yank a cluster of IED's from the ground by their detonating cords, opening the boot of an explosive-laden car by means of a hefty kick or letting off a smoke grenade as he approaches the danger zone \\"to create a diversion\\". There are plenty of other military mess-ups; some of these are clearly excusable cinematographic necessities (soldiers standing around in groups, M113 armoured personnel carriers instead of Bradleys and so-on). However, plenty of others are inexcusably sloppy and betray the absence of a decent (or any) military advisor. I was particularly offended when the three main characters rushed off alone and on foot into the hostile night-time suburbs to track down a bomber... and then split up because \\"we can cover more ground that way\\" for heavens' sake! If the US Army really operate like this (and I cannot convince my self that they do) there is hardly any surprise that the war has lasted so long and achieved so little as it has.<br /><br />In the end, this is a deeply ordinary film and it is prone to many of the usual war movie cliches; tensions between the maverick and his by-the-book colleague ultimately resolved, friendship with with the local street urchin, the race against time, shall I cut the red wire or the blue wire? oh the horror! oh the humanity! think of the children!<br /><br />Without the hype it would have made a fairly decent war movie and I would happily have given it three stars. Because of the hype, my sensibilities are offended and I can only give it two.<br /><br />If you really want to know what it is like to be an EOD officer in Iraq, there are plenty of decent memoirs - [[ASIN:0552155713 Eight Lives Down]] being a decent place to start.ds, opening the boot of an explosive-laden car by means of a hefty kick or letting off a smoke grenade as he approaches the danger zone \\"to create a diversion\\". 0061969656|2.0|I have read almost everything published which Bernard Cornwell has written.  If I were to review all of those other books, I would likely award an average of 4 stars.  So, it was with disappointment that I finished Death of Kings in what was only a 2 star experience.  Some reviewers have expressed confusion or a lack of direction in this latest book about the years leading up to the forming of England (around 900 AD).  I wasn't confused as much as I was uninterested.  This book felt too much like a hurried \\"bridge\\" in the overall narrative, as if it were filler for Cornwell to get through Alfred's death and onto the next phase of the history.  Perhaps it was.  It felt that way to me because the political environment is explored much less than in his other books in the same series (and others), because the antagonists are each much the same as the other (and none of them terribly interesting), and because the climactic battle scenes have become very formulaic.  The latter is hard to completely avoid as there is history to refer to to find some details of the actual battles, but the overwhelming-odds-overcome-by-last-minute-reinforcements is stale.  What is more, even though the history and even the book relate that these battles were,in fact, critical, the writing to that point was unconvincing.  Unlike most other Cornwell books, there lacked an urgency to the outcome.  Naturally, some of this is \\"knowing how the movie ends\\" through history or through safe assumption, but Cornwell is a wonderful writer and has on most other occasions pulled this off.  I'm hopeful of a much stronger, much more compelling effort in the next installment of the series.  The period he writes about is fascinating and he has written so well in the rest of the Uhtred stories.

B0067LY4WG|5.0|How do you access the important moments in your life?<br /><br />Not the moments the world sees.<br /><br />I mean the great personal moments, the ones that matter most.<br /><br />I'm certain I'm not alone when I say that music has been a direct pathway to my memory of these moments.<br /><br />Like this: I am not someone who stays up all night, ever, but when my first book was due and I was just 21 and so scared, I sat at the typewriter until dawn, playing Dylan's Blonde on Blonde over and over as I wrote, and then, as the business day started, I walked the manuscript to my publisher with a confidence I wish I felt every day. Ever since, whenever \\"Visions of Johanna\\" shows up....<br /><br />That memory is the exception. Most connected to music are connected also to women. The happy memories have a varied play list: The Four Tops, Otis Redding, Nusrat Fateh Ali Khan. The heavier memories --- almost all of them --- are linked to the songs of Leonard Cohen.<br /><br />If you're in the Cohen Cult, you have a list like mine. As someone has said, \\"You play Leonard after the lovers have left and are in the arms of others.\\" Not always. Sometimes the women were there, and so was a kind of distress you didn't understand and didn't particularly want but couldn't resist --- like a black-and-blue mark you can't help pressing, I used to think. There was something about that pain....<br /><br />The creator of those songs always knew all about that. He picked up the guitar as a kid because he sensed it could help him with girls. It worked --- Cohen is catnip to many of the women in my life. As Cohen now says, \\"It was agreeable to have some kind of a reputation or some kind of list of credentials so you didn't have to start from scratch with every woman you walked into.\\" Look around a Cohen concert and even now, when he says it doesn't matter, you'll see women who look at him as their romantic ideal.<br /><br />Leonard Cohen is now 77. A few years ago, his manager stole most of his money, so he went on a two-year tour, giving long shows that were as close to perfect as anything we're ever likely to see. Everyone who saw Cohen on that tour will hold tight to the image of a thin man in a gray suit, tipping his fedora to his audience in gratitude and humility as he delivered what might euphemistically be called his greatest hits.<br /><br />\\"I remember we were playing in Ireland and the reception was so warm that tears came to my eyes and I thought, 'I can't be seen weeping at this point,' then I turned around and saw the guitar player weeping.\\"<br /><br />When the tour was over, he went into the studio with 10 new songs and recorded a CD ironically titled \\"Old Ideas.\\" That too was bracing --- it's very likely he'll tour again and, perhaps for the last time, we'll see the dapper gent in the gray suit tip his fedora to us in humility and gratitude.<br /><br />I was not exactly looking forward to this CD. His last, \\"Dear Heather,\\" was a weak effort, a marker of decline. Worse than decline; it sounded as if he was borderline addled, incapable of realizing that he really shouldn't be releasing this.<br /><br />So...is this an old man's record? It would be easy to make the case. \\"I've got no future, I know my days are few,\\" he sings. \\"I thought the past would last me, but the darkness got that too.\\" As for thinking about death --- he practices Zen, you'd better believe his extinction has come up for him. \\"I've come to the conclusion, reluctantly, that I am going to die,\\" he told a recent interviewer. \\"So naturally those questions arise and are addressed. But, you know, I like to do it with a beat.\\"<br /><br />And yet \\"Old Ideas\\" is anything but a valedictory. It's taut, vital music, stripped of the electronic gimmicks that sounded so cool a few records ago and got tiresome when they seemed to be covering for weak songs and a weakening voice. You'll hear bits of JJ Cale here, and an echo of \\"Old Black Joe,\\" and, in the background, smart homages to his own work. (For the best example, listen to \\"Darkness.\\")<br /><br />That's the music. The lyrics are something else: considered, bone-deep, precise. And smart in a way that looks like his best work --- a reach for what is eternally true. Here's his method:<br /><br />\\"I don't really like songs with ideas. They tend to become slogans. They tend to be on the right side of things: ecology or vegetarianism or antiwar. All these are wonderful ideas but I like to work on a song until those slogans, as wonderful as they are and as wholesome as the ideas they promote are, dissolve into deeper convictions of the heart. I never set out to write a didactic song. It's just my experience. All I've got to put in a song is my own experience.\\"<br /><br />\\"My own experience\\" is precisely why we put on the headphones, light the candles, make ceremony out of listening. Because Leonard Cohen, for those who love him, is older than time and younger than tomorrow. He insists he has no answers --- \\"Who's to blame in this catastrophe? I never figured that out\\" --- but we know he does something even more important: He asks the right questions.precise. Because Leonard Cohen, for those who love him, is older than time and younger than tomorrow. He insists he has no answers --- \\"Who's to blame in this catastrophe? I never figured that out\\" --- but we know he does something even more important: He asks the right questions.

0316044695|5.0|I've read a number of eye-witness accounts of modern warfare and its heroes, and this one stands out for two main reasons--the man telling the story, and the fact that it all happened less than seven years ago. In June 2005, Marcus Luttrell (Team Leader, SDV TEam 1, Alfa Platon) is part of a four-man team sent into the wilderness mountains of the Pakistani border to kill or capture a single target: Ben Sharmak, notorious al Qaeda leader. Less than 24 hours later, Marcus is the only SEAL left alive. His fight on the mountain, his refuge with a local tribe, and his rescue by U.S. Army Rangers are laid out here in 450 pages of some of the most gripping, funny, appalling, and heartbreaking words I've ever read.  \\"I will never quit,\\" Marcus says, quoting the philosophy of the U.S. Navy SEALS. \\"If knocked down, I will get back up, every time. I will draw on every remaining ounce of strength to protect my teammates and to accomplish our mission. I am never out of the fight.\\" This a story of American soldiers--SEAL or no--who did just that. Read it for Marcus's gutsy honesty; his ironclad faith; his dry (but genuine) sense of humor that could give Patrick McManus a run for his money. Read it for his patriotism; his love for his brothers; his blood, toil, tears, and sweat. Read it for the same reason Marcus Luttrell wrote it--to remember our guys.

145162445X|1.0|This review is from: A Universe from Nothing (Hardcover)  Late in the book the author uses the phrase, \\"a deeper nothing,\\" as a final evocation of the source of creation. This is one of several examples in this book where we might have hoped for greater clarity. Since nothing is the subject of this book, we might expect to find a clear working definition of the word, but none is given. We are then left to surmise that the author makes use of the vacuum or empty space to signify nothing; the book then provides no insight on universal creation. It's recognized that the universe may have evolved from empty space, but this explanation leaves us with the question - \\"From whence came the empty space,\\" or equally \\"From whence the `deeper nothing'?\\"  Much of the author's picture of creation relies on unsubstantiated or speculative science. The first second in the history of the universe cannot be explained by known laws of physics. The inflationary universe agrees with astronomical observations, provided that dark energy and dark matter are included in the equation, and that spontaneous symmetry breaking is a correct theory of particle physics. But inflation in the early universe has astounding features, not the least of which is the existence of an intensely negative force that fuels the inflationary period and fades away in a split second. Also, Quantum Gravity is unknown; the proposed mediator of the gravitational force - the graviton - has never been detected, and gravity may be an entirely different force from the forces described by the Standard Model of particle physics. But the book's greatest weakness lies in its confused use of the word nothing.  Unfortunately, the word nothing has several meanings, but the one we're looking for in the context of creation is the absolute absence of being. It is that nothing that does not exist. Any discussion of a universe created from nothing will have little value if it fails to recognize a more relevant meaning of nothing.

B000IZ7YK6|5.0|Chinatown is a throwback noir thriller set in the broad daylight of southern California. The color palette is all primary: bright yellows and sanguinary reds-the opposite of what you'd expect from the typical noir thriller. Polanski sets the tone and the period perfectly. Unlike most noir films of the forties, though, Chinatown has a great script with fantastic characters, a whirling, looping (but always sensible and believable) plot, and one of the best endings of any Hollywood film of any period. Though it need not be said at this point, Nicholson is at his very best, and even the icy Faye Dunaway comes across as sympathetic. John Huston is a show stopper as a sociopathic multimillionaire. So if you like period pictures about big-time corruption, water shortages, and the seedy birth of a big city, rent Chinatown. It's one of the best pictures ever made. Robert Towne penned the keep you guessing script. Just another unparalleled film of the American seventies, a time when they seemed to come off the line as regularly as clockwork.

0743233832|5.0|The quality and the quantity of wisdom in this small book if fantastic. Marcus Aurelius didn't set out to write a book for fame or fortune; the entirety of the text he wrote to himself. There's something very compelling about a humble philosophy that attracted minds like those of both this author (one of \\"the five good Emperors\\" of Rome), and a lowly slave (as was Epictetus, another Stoic philosopher) to dedicate their lives to develop the same creed. It's actually easy to understand this fact when experiencing the sheer benevolence, justice, profound consideration for others, desire to better oneself, disdain of vice, strength to boldly weather Fate, and appreciation for rational thinking that overflows from each page of this book. Overall, a work that ought really be regarded much more widely, for which reason I would emphatically recommend it to anyone. If I could I'd give it more 'stars' in my rating.  To me this is the most succinct of the writings from Stoic philosophers that have been passed down to us through time; the topics are varied in thought and yet, to the point. His refreshing perspectives are often enlightening through their simplicity and rationality. The whole thing may even become for some, as it was for me, the stepping stone to delve into further study of Stoicism from the work of other philosophers.  I haven't compared this with other translations, so all I can say is that this one was easy to read, crisp, and engaging.

0811870413|5.0|I've been baking bread off and on for over 20 years, but I've always shied away from sourdough. Oh I've attempted it before, but my starter never was consistent and it seemed to be too much work to keep it going. However Tartine Bread finally got me going on sourdough breads and now I am hooked. The directions are very detailed yet easy to follow, plus the photos help. One reason why my starter may have worked well this time around is because I nurtured my starter for 3 weeks before attempting to make bread. The starter-making process is also one of the simplest ones to follow, compared to other cookbooks.  Robertson does mention retarding the dough in the refrigerator, but doesn't go into much detail about it. I found that the bread does better with a long final rise in the refrigerator (over 12 hours) and can go directly from the fridge into the oven. I get more oven spring if the dough is cold to begin with, plus it's easier to score the dough.  Other than that missing detail, the book goes to several lengths to explain the process and allowing for adjustments. The book also makes for excellent reading too about the author's bakery and his passion for bread.  For any one wanting to get into sourdough bread-making, this is the book to buy.

1594202842|4.0|Battle Hymn of the Tiger Mother is the brutally honest memoir of a Chinese-American mother, and how the Chinese parenting style, she used to raise her two daughters, is so different, and in her opinion, superior to the way most Western parents raise their children.  In the author's opinion, Western parents were too easy on their children, and too worried about their self-esteem.  The result is often soft-hearted, thin-skinned children instead of tough resilient ones.  Western parents, according to Chua, don't push their children enough to be the very best they can be, and she claims that they also allow their children to make too many of their own decisions.  According to Chua, Western children often select activities that she deems are not in the child's best interest. Examples of these are,  cheer leading, drama club or any other sport or activity that you can't win a medal in.<br /><br />Amy Chua and her husband Jed Rubenfeld, are both Yale Law professors and authors. The couple agreed early on that Amy would decide on the parenting style for their daughters, and as for religion, the girls would be raised Jewish like their father Jed, even though Amy was raised Catholic.<br /><br />High expectations, and respect for authority were a given, and the girls were raised to believe that \\"nothing is fun unless you are good at it\\". To achieve this, one had to work hard and endure long hours of practice, practice and more practice. After school the girls often had to practice up to (4) hours each evening.  Building confident and successful children was all that was important to this mother. When it came to studies, a grade of (A-) was unacceptable. The girls, Sophia, the oldest, and Lulu, the younger child, were never allowed to have play dates, sleep overs, watch television, be in a school play, play computer games, nor could they choose their own extracurricular activities. Friends outside of school were not allowed, as they had to fit them into a busy schedule, often  for a year in advance.  In school, not being #1 was never an option.  The only subjects that were not important to Amy for their daughters was gym and drama -- in Amy's world, if you couldn't win awards it wasn't something worth spending time on.<br /><br />Sophia was always respectful and eager to please her parents, she was tops in her class gifted as a Suzuki piano student.  She was subjected to rigorous lessons, special teachers and the girls even had to practice when the family vacationed.  Amy even chose hotels based on whether a piano would be available for Sophia to practice on.<br /><br />Lulu was also highly intelligent, and a talented violinist, but she was a rebellious child with a mind of her own. She was a girl who often went head to head with her mother with neither willing to back down.<br /><br />I'm not going to elaborate on the parenting methods Amy Chua used on her girls.  You probably wouldn't believe me, and you really should read this book for yourself.  At times while reading this book, I felt a bit like a voyeur, peeking into the windows and looking at the daily life of this family. Honestly, had that been the case, I probably would have called CPS (Child Protective Services) on at least a couple of occasions about this mother.<br /><br />Although her tough Tiger Mom style seemed, in most cases, to produce the results she was hoping for, I wonder what price the girls paid emotionally in their attempts to please their mother, and how she will be remembered by the girls later on.  It always seemed like nothing they did was good enough for their mother, and that they could have done better.  Even though the reader did learn a little about Jed, the girl's father, and the way he was raised as a child, I got the impression that Jed rarely interfered with the demands placed upon the girls by their mother.<br /><br />Do I recommend this book? Absolutely, even though I don't agree with her parenting style,  and I also thought she carried this huge chip on her shoulder.  Perhaps a blend of Chinese and Western parenting styles might be the answer to raising a smart, self-confident and sane child.styles might be the answer to raising a smart, self-confident and sane child.

(Remember the scene in the rec room, before the Thing gets free and Lars is petting his dog?) -that is supposed to be the \\"Thing\\" dog at the end since the Thing killed the dog after it's escape.<br /><br />Again, Carpenter's film isn't perfect but it's a masterpiece in horror and paranoia -the new film, should have had everyone on the edge of their seats with palatable tension but (except for not knowing what to expect) this movie didn't have the tension. The new film should have set the tone and taken it a step further -real adrenaline rushed paranoia.<br /><br />Another flub is when Kate (barely) escapes the other chick \\"Thing\\" and it attacks the poor doctor -suddenly, outta nowhere Lars comes running up with the flamethrower -? How did he know to bring it? In the '82 film, Childs was told to bring the flamethrower. The '11 movie assumes too much.<br /><br />I respect that they used many practical elements but some of the CGI actually looked like, well, CGI. The scene where Adam is attacked and the Thing is merging its face with his, its seemed to me they didn't finish that CGI very well, watch it a few times. I only rated it as high as I did outta respect for Carpenter's movie.<br /><br />The characters, though well played, looked too clean. The grit and dirt of the '82 film was lost (admittedly there is 29 years separating the two movies) but all the characters didn't look weather beaten or even cold. The girls looked too nice, the guys too sharp and clean. Not enough facial hair, unkempt or ill trimmed. It was too slick. The Thing attacks were too \\"one after the other\\" -no suspense. How did half the people (including the guy on the helicopter and the other chick) become Things? In the '82 movie, you can kind of get a timeline of when most everyone got attacked -not all mind you but most.<br /><br />In the end, if you're a fan of the '82 Carpenter film, stay that way -you may be disappointed in this prequel. I was. Again, I rated it three stars out of respect for what Carpenter did in 1982. He unleashed the Thing.

B006H3MIV8|5.0|This set is easily one of the best tribute albums I've ever heard.  Every time I listen to it, I marvel at the overall high quality.  The genius of Dylan's songwriting is not shown simply by the sheer number of artists who contributed to this album.  It's also reflected in the fact that so many musical genres are represented here:  rock, pop, folk, country, blues, soul, jazz, and even a beautiful instrumental by the Kronos Quartet.  Is it perfect?  No, there are a few missteps, but it's amazing that there are so many excellent performances on such a large set with so many diverse artists.  Let's get the bad songs out of the way:  4th Time Around - Not one of my favorite Dylan songs, and Oren Lavie's version is even more dull than the original.  Desolation Row - My Chemical Romance did a horrible cover of this song for the Watchmen soundtrack, and their live version here is just as bad.  The Lonesome Death of Hattie Carroll - This song is full of sorrow, but Cage the Elephant's performance couldn't be more flat and lifeless.  Absolutely disgraceful.  Forever Young - Pete Seeger talks, rather than sings, his way through this song.  A complete waste of time.  I'd Have You Anytime - Evan Rachel Wood performs it like a bad lounge act.  She should stick to acting.  With God On Our Side - K'naan leaves out some of the verses, changes some of the words, and adds a bunch of his own words between the verses.  The result is that he demonstrates very clearly that his lyrics are nowhere near being comparable to Dylan's, and the power of the song is vastly diluted by the radically changed words.  This is easily the worst song on the album.  Now for the best stuff.  Blind Willie McTell - Tom Morello gives a powerful performance with his gritty vocals and killer guitar.  You're Gonna Make Me Lonesome When You Go - Much to my surprise, Miley Cyrus does an excellent job.  Turns out she's a damn good singer.  Who knew?  Ring Them Bells - It's a truly beautiful song, and Natasha Bedingfield's gorgeous voice makes for a memorable cover.  Make You Feel My Love - Well, of course Adele nails it.  Duh.  The Times They Are A-Changin' - Flogging Molly rocks the hell out of this.  Don't Think Twice, It's All Right - I'm sure this is the most polarizing song on the album, but I think it's incredible.  Ke$ha sounds absolutely wrecked, as if she just watched a family member die before recording the song.  In the original version, Dylan is trying to play it off as if the end of a relationship is no big deal.  The genius of Ke$ha's version is that she's saying that it's no big deal, but she's clearly destroyed by the situation.  It's incredibly powerful.  There are many other memorable songs on this album.  Even without the six bad performances and Dylan's original \\"Chimes of Freedom\\", that leaves more than sixty songs which range from good to excellent.  It's for a good cause, and you can't beat the price.  So why haven't you bought it yet?

B0036T31YY|5.0|Time travel itself dates back long before H.G. Wells had penned his first science fiction novel the brilliant \\"The Time Machine\\" but \\"time loops\\" are a more recent sub-genre of time travel made popular by \\"Ground Hog Day\\". GHD has even become a term in Quantum Mechanics and was mentioned in \\"Triangle\\". The basic theme of time loops is that the hero/heroes relive the same period of time over and over in hopes to make changes which might make a better future. Jess, the heroine of the story, is reliving an ocean voyage and is the only one of her friends who realizes they are caught in a time loop. The greatest problem with time loop stories is the they create faults which cancel out the logic of the story such as the famous Grandfather Paradox where a person kills his own grandfather making it impossible for him to have existed and travel back in time to kill his own grandfather. As one person pointed out the looped car accident could not have happened over and over or else there would have been a pile of broken cars and bodies, the accumulation shown by the pile of dead sea gulls. However, anomalies do occur in the story, for example, in one loop Jess never interacts with the new group while in another loop she holds a shotgun on her other self. The traffic accident could have been one of these anomalies and not part of the loop. I defend the movies because I really loved it and consider it one of the better, perhaps the best, in this new genre.

0385534639|5.0|Lots of books refer to magic (in the deep, not stage-illusionist, sense) or include events that are powered by magic, but very few give a feeling of what magic really is--in a sense, are magic themselves. This book is one of them. Even the cover is right; its colors are minimal from most angles, but seen in the right angle and light, the decorative swirls around the title take on an iridescent glow.  The book concerns a man and a woman, raised from childhood by their fathers/mentors to be rivals in a magical contest (for the glory of the older men's egos), who instead fall in love when they finally meet. Marco and Celia are worth knowing, as are the older magicians, the members of the Night Circus, and the circus's biggest fan, Herr Thiessen. The real star of the book, however, is the circus itself. Created to be the venue for the magical contest, it instead becomes the venue for Marco and Celia's courtship and, ultimately, their chief reason for existence. It is truly, as its alternate title says, the Circus of Dreams. The author draws readers into the circus from the beginning, making us feel we are actually inside the striped tents, and describes the wonderful acts in considerable detail. Her use of the present tense throughout adds to the feeling of immediacy.  This is the best fantasy book I have read in a very long time, and several not-easily-impressed friends I have shared it with say the same thing. I can't wait to see what this author will do next.

The awful movie cliches that abound in that other disaster film Volcano are absent from Dante's Peak. You use alcohol to dull the pain whenever life doesn't seem to be going your way. You have no issues with getting completely wasted at any social gathering you attend. You aren't even of legal age. You are nineteen and a freshman in college, but you have no problem using your fake id to give you instant access to alcohol at what seems like every single moment of this novel.<br /><br />But that's not all. You are addicted to gambling as well. You may say that you are trying to start a new life away from the gambling addict issues of your father, but it seems as if you are placing bets at every corner. For goodness sakes, the entire premise of this novel begins with a ridiculous bet you make with Travis! Instead of dealing with the gambling addiction your father suffers from as well, you feed his very problem by agreeing to solve his latest gambling pickle of a situation by GAMBLING to get him out of his troubles. How hypocritical of you to run away from Travis for hinting at a desire to get involved in this life to earn money when you are constantly finding yourself making similar choices.<br /><br />You obviously have some serious issues that stem from your difficult childhood with an alcoholic mother and a gambling father who blames you for his terrible streak of bad luck. That is understandable that you would need to work through those issues. So work through them. Get some counseling -- beyond this letter, obviously. Your constant changes in your relationship with Travis have some deep seeded reasons for them. Instead of having such a tumultuous relationship it might have been helpful for you to get through your own issues before dealing with Travis's.<br /><br />And as for that relationship, well . . . But then I would scold myself because what you have, sister, is a borderline abusive, and definitely codependent relationship. He goes out and gets two huge tattoos without thinking twice about it. He has his own violent tendencies that really need to be addressed in counseling (but I'll spare adding a letter to him to this review). He beats men to a pulp constantly if they so much as look at you too closely or make a stupid comment of innuendo. How did none of these guys end up in the hospital or dead? And how did Travis not end up in jail? Anyway, the truth is that you should be much more wary about getting into a relationship with a man such as this. At least give it a bit more time before you make any long term commitments. All abusive people are able to be fun and charming some of the time. It takes time to see if they truly have changed. And even if you can put aside his tendency to smash other people's faces in, shouldn't you be a little more cautious about pushing so quickly into a relationship with a man who has never kept a relationship longer than a night. I'm sure that the ending was meant to seem romantic, but I couldn't help but think that it was a crazy decision for you to have made on impulse. I understand your new recognition of your feelings for Travis, but really, sweetie, you should give yourself some time to process things before you head out on a jet plane to change your life forever. Too quick of a decision.<br /><br />So overall I have to say that it was easy to read your story and root for your relationship to succeed, but you were just making too many mistakes for me to feel good while I was reading. I would never, ever use your life or decisions as an example for what I would want for my daughters, or my friends, or anyone really. The clip system is ONLY compatible with Streamline Darts.<br /><br />The Weight of this is less than the Stampede or Vulcan, but higher than the Recon, which is a very similar weapon. I'm not very strong and enjoy this for home or office use, but will consider carefully before toting it all over campus again. 0307592731|4.0|This book is a highly enjoyable addition to the literature of outdoor adventures-cum-vision quest / walkabout genre. It reminded me of the Page Stegner book I read recently, [[ASIN:1582435375 Adios Amigos: Tales of Sustenance and Purification in the American West]], because the authors, in both cases, were given an opportunity to review their shortcomings, and to stretch beyond their limits.  It might be said that all good adventures provide such experiences; however, the level of intimacy these two writers share with their readers does make these books stand out in my mind.  Cheryl Strayed's adventure took place on the Pacific Crest Trail in 1995, a year that saw an abundance of snow that made the going difficult, and even impossible, through certain segments of the trail.  The hardships of the trail taught her humility.  She learned that, despite her knowledge of camping outdoors, she didn't know much about hiking, or how it differed from walking.  She had much to learn from others along the way!  Yet, her perseverance in the face of the challenges she met on the trail, which made quitting appealing, resulted in greater self-esteem and self-confidence.  Solo journeys bring to the fore the bigger picture struggles of one's life, if only because there is no one with you to divert your attention.  I admired Ms. Stayed discipline to that end, creating her solitude even as she enjoyed the camaraderie on the trail by purposefully limiting contact with others primarily to campgrounds along the way.  This enabled her to gain insight on the choices she had made for her life, the relationships that had caused her pain, some of the reasons for her drug use, and her responsibility to herself to move forward in her life.  Among her insights, the one that amused me most was her learning that the universe is never joking!  Another thing that struck me was her coming to terms with \\"not knowing,\\" and her compassion for her younger self.  She wanted, as we all do, to know the answers to the \\"why's\\" of our lives:  why did her mother die, why did what remained of her family seem to fall apart, etc.  Her conclusions are insightful: simple but profound, and perhaps exactly the conclusion that we all need draw from our own curious circumstances.  She writes from the perspective of 15 years later, having taken her walkabout in 1995.  This perspective provided a space in her heart for the child she once was, rather than continuing to harbor regrets, or 'beating herself up.'  This is not a how-to book but, if you are not a seasoned hiker and are planning a long hiking trip, solo or not, this book will help you to plan for success.  She has made almost all the mistakes for you!  And you will know from the beginning of your trip that all people who take this sort of journey run into unexpected challenges and even hardships.  You won't need the same amount of reassurance Ms. Strayed did from fellow travelers.  You will know that your struggles are not unique, and that your persistence will pay off in the long run.  A cautionary note:  the author acknowledges that, as a solo female, she was very fortunate that most acquaintances made on the trail were kind and helpful people.  She listened to her gut when they were not, and escaped unscathed.  Her decision to go it alone initially, with \\"no fear,\\" was based on her belief that her mother's death was the worst thing that could ever happen to her.  However, as she hiked, she did realize that she was taking a great risk that could have dire consequences, whether a result of her own misstep or an encounter with predators, human or otherwise.  As difficult as it might be to find a compatible companion, a companion provides a measure of safety that could make a difference between life and death.  Reading Wild will put you on the trail with Ms. Strayed, and I highly recommend it!  I looked forward to joining her every evening, just as she seemed to look forward crawling into her sleeping bag with a book each evening!  Her books were part of the story:  She provides a reading list to share with her readers!  While I felt her pain from her raw and blistered feet, and I shared her mental anguish as she burned segments of the books to keep the weight down to a minimum, having initially overburdened herself with her packing.  If you enjoy this subject matter and want to focus on \\"women's outdoor adventures,\\" two very good books from the 1990's, [[ASIN:1580050069 Gifts of the Wild: A Woman's Book of Adventure (Adventura Books)]] and [[ASIN:1878067303 Another Wilderness: Notes from the New Outdoorswoman (Adventura Books)]], are worth your time.  Another book I came across that might be of interest is [[ASIN:B001G8WJLS Going Alone: Women's Adventures in the Wild (Adventura Books)]].

0060936223|4.0|Powerful, poignant memoir by legendary rock poet Patti Smith about her shared struggles to succeed as an artist with the controversial photographer Robert Mapplethorpe when they were, in her words, \\"just kids,\\" starting out as starving artists in the seedy underbelly of the late 1960's-early 1970's NYC. It's at times lyrically written, though not always as insightful or as revealing as one might like - we get next to nothing about how she came to the song by Bruce Springsteen that would make her legend, \\"Because the Night\\" - but it reads like Forrest Gump at times, with an unending stream of famous names that flash across her life: Allen Ginsburg buying her dinner at an Automat, thinking she's a guy; Janis Joplin crying on her lap because the guy she fancied left with a prettier girl; Jimi Hendrix talking about his dreams for making music on a stairway just before departing for his fateful departure to London; and a plethora of literary, music and art world notables of the mid-20th Century. As much as she was an early muse and lover for Mapplethorpe, who comes across as pure and innocent, though recklessly, and ultimately fatally, pursuing his own inner muse, we do get an intimate picture of the tortured artist effect, of the great effort it takes to produce art, poetry and/or music that moves and inspires others. Ultimately, of course, this book is a love story, requited, but tragic between two young people who always supported each other, held on loosely, and let the other flower as their life's pursuits took them in different but parallel directions over time.

B005LAIGOQ|2.0|At the tail end of the 19th century, Arthur Kipps (Daniel Radcliffe) still feels like the wound is still fresh when it comes to his wife who died four years ago after giving birth to their son. Arthur is drowning in a depressive state that even his son notices. As a lawyer, Arthur is forced to travel to a secluded town to take care of the work of the now deceased Alice Drablow as a last ditch effort to save his job. Arthur is expecting to be buried in paperwork until the weekend, but the odd occurrences that transpire are a bit out of left field. Everyone in town practically begs for Arthur to return to London immediately as children continue to die gruesome deaths left and right. Ignoring their pleas, Arthur decides to confront the problem head on and stay at the Drablow's menacing house where his constant run-ins with the supernatural eventually take its toll on him.<br /><br />The Woman in Black is a horror film that I wanted to be good. It's Daniel Radcliffe's first movie outside of the Harry Potter franchise and he's been pretty enthusiastic about it in interviews, but every bit of promotional material seemed to point at the movie being your everyday, generic, run of the mill, \\"scary\\" movie. The Woman in Black does provoke your interest at first. The strange opening is a little hokey, but kind of intrigues you at the same time. The atmosphere the movie tries to setup is its strongest asset though. The heavy use of fog and old fashioned feel of the town does make the town feel like it existed in the late 1800s and the ominous score does its best to try and bring you to the edge of your seat. The Drablow house is the key to that atmosphere as it's absolutely gorgeous in this hideously grotesque kind of way. Everything is so dusty and creepy while the Victorian design only adds to that uneasy feeling the movie tries to stir up in the pit of your stomach.<br /><br />In the meantime though, everything else in the movie is working against it. It's extremely uneventful. Daniel Radcliffe reads papers, walks through a house, holds a candle, and gets a little dirty. That's the entire movie in one sentence. The Woman in Black also resorts to relying on nothing more than jump scares to try and scare its audience. There are four in the first twenty minutes; two from the same sequence and there are at least ten throughout the entire movie. Jump scares can be fantastic in small portions, but come off as incredibly weak when you can see them coming a mile away and are strung together haphazardly for a cheap effect. There isn't much dialogue while Arthur is in the Drablow house either, which is practically the entire movie. This was probably done to try and make the audience more absorbed with what was taking place on screen, but seems like a bit of a copout overall. Radcliffe has proven that he is an extremely talented actor, but he's pretty bland here. He mostly wanders around in a daze with a frazzled look on his face the entire time. His hosting gig on Saturday Night Live was more impressive in comparison.<br /><br />The Woman in Black will still be a very successful film as nearly everyone who was or still is a Harry Potter fan will be lining up around every street corner just to be able to see this movie, but the fact of the matter is that it just isn't a great movie. Its representation of the late 19th century is pretty good, but the writing, the \\"scares,\\" and (most of all) the entire conclusion are all just extremely disappointing. The Woman in Black is a watered down version of last year's Insidious that will more than likely gain a lot of praise it doesn't deserve.reads papers, walks through a house, holds a candle, and gets a little dirty. B006Z7Z3S6|4.0|Movies revolving around the supernatural have always felt lacking. Of course, the most recent ones are mostly remakes so they already have an uphill battle ahead of them but there are very few movies featuring ghosts or the supernatural that I feel are worth mentioning in a conversation about great films. Ghostbusters, The Orphanage, The Shining, Shutter (the Thai original), The Ring, The Frighteners, and The Devil's Backbone are about it for me. It's a sub genre of horror that just hasn't meshed well with me over the years much like exorcism films. In the same breath, I still haven't been able to get a clear read on what I think of Ti West as a writer and a director. The House of the Devil was really disappointing. Its slow pace made the film seem practically uneventful and didn't really feel worthwhile in the long run. The Innkeepers has a similar pace as The House of the Devil yet feels slightly more methodical on West's part in comparison.<br /><br />Luke (Pat Healy) and Claire (Sara Paxton) are the only two employees working during the final days of the Yankee Pedlar Inn. This hotel is rumored to be haunted by Madeline O' Malley, a woman who hung herself in her room after being stood up by her fianc&eacute; at the altar. Luke and Claire try to make contact with the paranormal through EVP recording devices in between watching the front desk and handling the few stragglers who come to stay during the hotel's final weekend of operation. Needless to say, Luke and Claire begin to see results as the guests at the hotel become a bit stranger.<br /><br />I'm surprised the score to The Innkeepers was as good as it was. It's a little bizarre to have such good things to say about movies coming out in between January and March since these are the months that studios decide to push whatever's been sitting on their shelf for a long period of time or release something they expect to do poorly at the box office. The score is really fantastic though, especially during the opening credits. It's usually very strings heavy and puts you on the edge of your seat on more than one occasion. It helps add that extra bit of tension. At other times, a lack of music speaks volumes. The way the film encompasses the importance of sound into the overall experience of the film is pretty extraordinary. Suddenly listening to a film is just as important (if not slightly more so) as watching it.<br /><br />The camera work is really spectacular, as well. Slow, winding shots make it seem as though you're grudgingly snooping around the corner along with the characters on screen. The camera's pace as it travels through the hotel's hallways make you feel like you're walking through it yourself. There are also several shots directly behind Luke or Claire that feel very third person. It's just extremely solid camera work that's more stunning than you may be expecting.<br /><br />The Innkeepers won't be for everyone though as it's incredibly slow moving. It crawls at almost a snail's pace, but it's the little things that keep you interested. Everything is very dialogue driven as Luke and Claire play tricks on each other and talk smack about each guest that comes to stay at the hotel. Claire's ghost story about Madeline O' Malley is around the time things really get interesting and Leanne's (Kelly McGillis) pendulum speech make the smallest things seem larger than they really are. The Innkeepers spends every expense establishing this thick, creepy atmosphere and is the prime definition of a slow burn at its finest.<br /><br />The Innkeepers may seem a little dull on the surface, but all it needs is a chance to let its layers unravel right before your eyes. Character development and a horror film that isn't in your face showing you every gratuitous and gory detail is almost unheard of anymore. With its unsettling score, its superb cinematography, and engaging script, The Innkeepers delivers a rare horror gem that's a breath of fresh air to the genre.ery strings heavy and puts you on the edge of your seat on more than one occasion.
